","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_154","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_154","title":"The Constitutional Framework for War ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_437","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_437","title":"Where are the “War Powers”? ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_438","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_438","title":"There is no specific provision for “war powers” in the United States Constitution. What we have come in subsequent times to understand as the Constitution’s “war powers” rests in a cluster of clauses spread over two articles, thus dividing the “war powers” between the executive and the legislative.  Article II, Sec. 2, provides that the president “shall be Commander-in-Chief of the Army and Navy of the United States, and of the Militia of the several States, when called into the actual Service of the United States.” Article I, Sec. 8, vests in Congress the authority to “declare war,” and it is necessary to examine the Commander-in-Chief Clause in concert with that provision. The Framers, after all, created a system of integrated parts and powers. To what degree, if any, are war powers shared? To what degree may the executive and legislative branches act independently in the exercise of American military power? More specifically, does the Commander in Chief Clause empower the president to unilaterally initiate war or lesser military hostilities? In attempting answers to these and related questions, we will explore the debates in the Constitutional Convention, state ratifying conventions, the Federalist papers and other contemporaneous writings, Supreme Court decisions rendered at the dawn of the Republic, and early presidential interpretations of the Constitution. Let us explore the Framers’ blueprint for matters of war and peace, and how they envisioned the integration of the War Clause and the Commander in Chief Clause. The Framers’ decision to withhold from the president the power to commence war signaled a dramatic departure from the existing models of government that placed the war power, indeed virtually all foreign affairs powers, in the hands of the executive. In The Second Treatise of Government (1690), John Locke had described three branches of government: legislative, executive, and federative. According to Locke, the federative power, or what we today refer to as the power to conduct foreign affairs, encompassed “the power of war and peace, leagues and alliances, and all the transactions withal persons and communities without the commonwealth.” The power, moreover, was “almost always united” with the executive. The separation of the executive and federative powers, Locke warned, would bring “disorder and ruin.” Similarly, Sir William Blackstone, in his distinguished Commentaries on the Laws of England (1765-1769), had explained that the king enjoyed absolute power over foreign affairs and war: the authority to send and receive ambassadors, make treaties and alliances, make war or peace, issue letters of marque and reprisal, command the military, raise and regulate fleets and armies, and represent the nation in its intercourse with foreign governments. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide15816","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Authority vs. Power","thumbnail":"dr/Slide15816_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si15850","si15852","Text_Caption_115","Text_Caption_116","btn_previous_77","si31835","si31860","btn_next_78","si31914","si31939","Image_700","Image_701","Text_Caption_691"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide15816"}}},{"id":"si15930","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si15932","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_38","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_38","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_24","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_24","title":"Separation of Powers ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_117","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_117","title":"A “Fourth Branch” of Government ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_118","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_118","title":"Despite their familiarity with the English scheme of governance, the Framers, in what can be described as an uncommon move, granted Congress the greatest share of the nation’s foreign affairs powers. Congress was vested with the sole and exclusive authority to initiate war. The Constitution withheld from the president the sole power to make treaties, making him share that power with the Senate. James Madison and Alexander Hamilton referred to this combination as a “fourth branch” of government, which they expected would manage most of the country’s foreign policy responsibilities. The president was granted the authority to receive ambassadors, an act that entails important international legal obligations. But the founding trio of Madison, Hamilton, and Jefferson did not view the Reception or “Recognition” Clause as a font of discretionary executive power to decide whether the United States would have relations with other nations or to determine unilaterally the tone and temper of those relations. Rather, the Framers understood the recognition power as a narrow, clerk-like function or duty imposed as a matter of convenience on the president, rather than Congress, to carry out the nation’s obligation under international law to receive ambassadors from sovereign countries. The president was granted the authority to send ambassadors abroad but only after the Senate approved his nominations. He was not vested with the monarchical power to issue letters of marque and reprisal; that power was granted to Congress. Nor was he given the authority to raise and regulate fleets and armies. That power, too, was left with Congress. Finally, Congress was also granted plenary authority over foreign commerce. An early draft reported by the Committee of Detail vested Congress with the power to “make war.” This bore sharp resemblance to the Articles of Confederation, which had placed the “sole and exclusive right and power of determining on peace and war” in the Continental Congress. Charles Pinckney, who expected Congress to meet only once a year, objected to the plan because he thought the legislative proceedings “were too slow” to protect the security interests of the nation. The draft also proved unsatisfactory to James Madison and Elbridge Gerry. In a joint resolution, they moved to substitute “declare” for “make,” “leaving to the Executive the power to repel sudden attacks.” The meaning of the motion is unmistakable. Congress was granted the power to make—that is, to initiate—war; the president, for obvious reasons, would be empowered to act immediately to repel sudden attacks without authorization from Congress. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_121","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_121","title":"There was no quarrel whatever with respect to the sudden attack provision, but there was some question as to whether the substitution of “declare” for “make” would effectuate the intention of Madison and Gerry. Roger Sherman of Connecticut thought the joint motion “stood very well,” saying, “The Executive shd. Be able to repel and not commence war.” George Mason of Virginia was against giving the power of war to the Executive, because he was not “to be trusted with it.” He was for “clogging rather than facilitating war.” The textual change was approved. Rufus King of Massachusetts furnished an explanation: “‘make’ war might be understood to ‘conduct’ it, which was an executive function,” a function reserved to the Commander-in-Chief.   The debates and the vote on the war power make it clear that Congress alone possesses the authority to initiate war. The war-making power was specifically withheld from the president; he was given only the authority to repel sudden attacks. James Wilson, who played a role only slightly less important than Madison’s in the Constitutional Convention, told the Pennsylvania ratifying convention: “This system will not hurry us into war; it is calculated to guard against it. It will not be in the power of a single man . . . to involve us in such distress; for the important power of declaring war is vested in the legislature at large.” Similar assurances were provided to other state ratifying conventions. He “was for vesting the power in the President, who will have all the requisite qualities, and will not make war but when the Nation will support it.” This drew from Elbridge Gerry the rejoinder that he “never expected to hear in a republic a motion to empower the Executive alone.” Butler’s view received no second, which was not surprising, given the fact that several Framers in the course of discussion on June 1, had emphasized their opposition to a presidential war power. The very notion, it seemed, smacked of monarchism and the royal prerogative and, as a consequence, engendered fear and resentment. But give Butler credit for being a quick study. By the end of the August 17 debate on the War Clause, Butler clearly understood the Framers’ decision to place the war power under legislative control, as evidenced by his motion “to give the Legislature power of peace, as they were to have that of war.” The motion, which represented an about-face on Butler’s part, drew no discussion, and it failed by a vote of 10-0. In all likelihood, it was viewed by delegates as utterly superfluous given the understanding that the war power encompassed authority to decide on matters of war and peace. By the end of the Philadelphia Convention, then, there was no support among the delegates for a presidential power to initiate war.  Butler continued his effort to distance himself from his “one-hour” advocacy of an executive war-making power. At the South Carolina ratifying convention on January 16, 1788, Butler explained to his fellow delegates the nature of the Constitutional Convention’s debate on the war power. He said, “Some gentlemen were inclined to give this power to the President; but it was objected to, as throwing into his hands the influence of a monarch, having an opportunity of involving his country in a war whenever he wished to promote her destruction.” Butler failed to mention that he had been the only advocate of a presidential war power. ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_124","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_124","title":"Sharing Power to Control its Abuse ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_125","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_125","title":"The Constitutional Convention’s rejection of giving the president the war-making power alone was attributable to two principal factors. First, the Framers were attached to republican ideology, the core principle of which is collective decision making in domestic as well as foreign affairs. Second, the Founders, influenced by their own experience under King George III, whom they regarded as a tyrant, and by their understanding of history, lived in fear of a powerful executive, and were adamantly opposed to a president’s unilateral control of foreign policy. History had its claims. In Federalist No. 75, Hamilton stated: “The history of human conduct does not warrant that exalted opinion of human virtue which would make it wise in a nation to commit interests of so delicate and momentous a kind, as those which concern its intercourse with the rest of the world, to the sole disposal of . . . a President.” In a letter to Jefferson in 1798, Madison observed:   “The constitution supposes, what the History of all Govts demonstrates, that the Ex. Is the branch of power most interested in war & most prone to it. It has accordingly with studied care, vested the question of war in the Legisl.”   The Framers’ decision to create a radically new blueprint for the conduct of foreign affairs justified Wilson’s remark that it was incorrect to consider “the Prerogatives of the British Monarch as a proper guide in defining the Executive powers. Some of these prerogatives were of a Legislative nature. Among others that of war and peace.” ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_85","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_85","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32467","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32492","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_86","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_86","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32546","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32571","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_703","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_703","roles":{}},{"id":"Image_704","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_704","roles":{}},{"id":"Text_Caption_694","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_694","title":"King Henry V at the Battle of Agincourt. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide16143","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Fear of a Powerful Executive","thumbnail":"dr/Slide16143_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si16177","si16179","Text_Caption_124","Text_Caption_125","btn_previous_85","si32467","si32492","btn_next_86","si32546","si32571","Image_703","Image_704","Text_Caption_694"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide16143"}}},{"id":"si16257","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si16259","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_42","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_42","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_28","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_28","title":"The British “Commander in Chief” ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_126","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_126","title":"A Model for the Framers ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_127","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_127","title":"The separation of the power to initiate war from the power to conduct it, reflected the interplay of different values. The decision to commence war requires the most solemn, deliberative debate. It emphasizes the values of collective decision making. But the decision of how to conduct war, to determine strategy and tactics, pivots on a different value, that of efficiency. In Federalist No. 74, Hamilton adduced the basic reason for making the president Commander-in-Chief: the direction of war “most peculiarly demands those qualities which distinguish the exercise of power by a single hand.” The power of directing a war and emphasizing the common strength “forms a usual and essential part in the definition of the executive authority.”  While the Framers rejected the Locke-Blackstone model for foreign affairs, they nevertheless drew their conception of the Commander in Chief Clause directly from English history. The office of Commander in Chief has never carried the power of war and peace. The title of Commander in Chief was introduced by King Charles I in 1639 in one of England’s wars with the Scots, and has always been used as a generic term referring to the highest officer in a particular chain of command. With the eruption of the English Civil Wars, both the King and Parliament appointed commanders in chief in various theaters of battle. The ranking Commander in Chief, purely a military post, was always under the command of a political superior, whether by appointed by the King, Parliament or, with the development of the cabinet system in the 18th century, by the secretary of war.  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_87","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_87","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32625","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32650","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_88","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_88","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32704","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32729","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_705","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_705","roles":{}},{"id":"Image_706","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_706","roles":{}},{"id":"Text_Caption_695","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_695","title":"Dutch image of Oliver Cromwell as usurper of monarchical power. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide16223","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The British \"Commander in Chief\"","thumbnail":"dr/Slide16223_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si16257","si16259","Text_Caption_126","Text_Caption_127","btn_previous_87","si32625","si32650","btn_next_88","si32704","si32729","Image_705","Image_706","Text_Caption_695"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide16223"}}},{"id":"si16337","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si16339","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_43","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_43","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_29","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_29","title":"Thomas Gage and Abuses of Power ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_128","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_128","title":"Commanders in Chief in the Colonies ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_129","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_129","title":"England transplanted the title to America in the 18th century by appointing a number of commanders in chief and by the practice of entitling colonial governors as commanders in chief (or occasionally as vice admirals or captains general). The appointment of General Thomas Gage as Commander in Chief from 1763 to 1776 caused the colonists grave concern, for he proceeded to interfere in civil affairs and acquired considerable influence over Indian relations, trade, and transportation. The bitter memory of his decision to quarter troops in civilians’ homes spawned the 3rd Amendment to the Constitution. These activities and others prompted the colonists in the Declaration of Independence to complain of King George III that he had “affected to render the Military Independent of and superior to the Civil Power.”   But the colonists had little reason to fear the governors who were given the title Commander in Chief (though they might resent them for ruling in London‘s interest), since the colonial assemblies claimed and asserted the right to vote funds for the militia as well as to call it into service. In fact, grievances came from the governors, who complained of the relative impotence of their positions. The colonists’ assemblies (and later, the states’) assertions of the power of the purse as a check on the Commander in Chief reflected an English practice that was instituted in the middle of the 17th century. By 1665, Parliament, as a means of maintaining political control of the military establishment, had inaugurated the policy of making annual appropriations lasting but one year.  This practice sharply emphasized the power of Parliament to determine the size of the army to be placed under the direction of the Commander in Chief.  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_89","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_89","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32783","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32808","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_90","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_90","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32862","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32887","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_707","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_707","roles":{}},{"id":"Text_Caption_696","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_696","title":"General Thomas Gage, Governor of the Province of Massachusetts Bay. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide16303","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Thomas Gage and Abuses of Power","thumbnail":"dr/Slide16303_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si16337","si16339","Text_Caption_128","Text_Caption_129","btn_previous_89","si32783","si32808","btn_next_90","si32862","si32887","Image_707","Text_Caption_696"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide16303"}}},{"id":"si16417","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si16419","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_44","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_44","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_30","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_30","title":"George Washington as Commander in Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_130","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_130","title":"The Commander in Chief during Crisis ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_131","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_131","title":"The Continental Congress continued the usage of the Commander in Chief title in 1775, when it unanimously decided to appoint George Washington as General. His commission named him “General and Commander in Chief, of the Army of the United Colonies.” While he was required to comply with orders and directions from Congress, he was invested by a Congress on the run with the powers of a dictator, to defeat the enemy. He become the source of all war-time decisions, the taxing/confiscation authority, and he became the director of public health and civil justice. And while Washington kept up his reports to Congress, and importantly returned his Commission after the war was won, his war-time empowerment is the predicate for expansive war-making power later. From the beginning, even though the executive was instructed by the legislature, crisis empowered the executive as an extension of the Commander in Chief role.  The practice of entitling the office at the apex of the military hierarchy as Commander in Chief and of the subordinating the office to a political superior, whether a King, Parliament, or Congress, had been firmly established for a century and a half and was thoroughly familiar to the Framers when they met in Philadelphia. Perhaps this settled historical usage accounts for the fact that there was little discussion of the Commander in Chief Clause at the Convention.  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_91","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_91","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32941","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si32966","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_92","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_92","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si33020","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si33045","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_708","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_708","roles":{}},{"id":"Image_709","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_709","roles":{}},{"id":"Text_Caption_697","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_697","title":"General George Washington. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide16383","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"George Washington as Commander in Chief","thumbnail":"dr/Slide16383_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si16417","si16419","Text_Caption_130","Text_Caption_131","btn_previous_91","si32941","si32966","btn_next_92","si33020","si33045","Image_708","Image_709","Text_Caption_697"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide16383"}}},{"id":"si16497","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si16499","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_45","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_45","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_31","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_31","title":"Without Debate and Without Explanation ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_132","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_132","title":"During the Convention ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_133","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_133","title":"In the plan that he read to the Convention on May 29, South Carolinian Charles Pinckney introduced the title of president and proposed, “He shall, by Virtue of his Office, be Commander in Chief of the Land Forces of U.S. and Admiral of their Navy.” Presumably, Pinckney had drawn on the traditional usage of the title employed in the South Carolina Constitution of 1776, which had provided for a “president and commander in chief,” and that of 1778, which had included a provision for a “governor and commander in chief.” There was no such plan or provision in the Randolph (or Virginia) Plan, which was read to the Convention on the same day. The New Jersey Plan, introduced by William Paterson on June 15, did not conclude whether the executive should consist of a single person or several. But it did resolve that the executive was “to direct all military operations,” and that none of the persons composing the federal executive “shall on any occasion take command of any troops, so as personally to conduct any enterprise as General, or in other capacity.” The qualifying clause was meant to discourage a military takeover of the government. When Hamilton submitted a plan to the Convention on June 18, as taken down by Madison, he probably did not propose the title Commander in Chief, but he undoubtedly had it in mind when he said the president was “to have the direction of war when authorized or begun.” The words Commander in Chief were adopted by the Convention without debate and without explanation. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_135","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_135","title":"Hamilton’s speech summarized the essence of the president’s powers as Commander in Chief: when war is authorized or begun, the president is to command the military operations of American forces. He elaborated on this theme and sharply distinguished the powers of the King and those of the president in Federalist No. 69, and it is a fair deduction that Hamilton’s discussion here captured the matured conclusions of the Convention. As Commander-in-Chief, Hamilton wrote, the president’s authority would be “much inferior” to that of the British King; “it would amount to nothing more than the supreme command and direction of the military and naval forces, as first General and admiral … while that of the British King extends to the declaring of war and to the raising and regulating of fleets and armies—all which by the Constitution … would appertain to the legislature.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_136","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_136","title":"The sharply limited role assigned to the president reflected what Madison called an axiom, an “axiom that the executive is the department of power most distinguished by its propensity to war; hence it is the practice of all states, in proportion as they are free, to disarm this propensity of its influence.” The Convention’s design of the War Clause, as James Wilson explained, was to prevent a “single man from “hurrying” us into war.” It was left to Madison who, in a letter to Thomas Jefferson that he wrote in 1798, eloquently explained in the rationale for separating the authority to initiate war from the power to conduct it. “Those who are to conduct a war,” wrote Madison, “cannot in the nature of things, be proper or safe judges, whether a war ought to be commenced, continued or concluded.” ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_137","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_137","title":"Early Practice and Understanding ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_138","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_138","title":"Nineteenth-century presidential practice and judicial decisions help to shape an understanding of the war power and the president’s role as Commander in Chief. Early presidents often refused to initiate hostilities without prior authorization from Congress. In 1792 and 1793, President George Washington received urgent requests for the use of military force from governors who feared impending attacks by Native Americans. Washington stated that he had no authority to order an attack and deferred the issue to Congress, “who solely are vested with the powers of war.” Contrary to the charge that President John Adams acted unilaterally in the Quasi-War with France of 1798-1800, Congress, in fact, passed some 20 statutes to authorize the war. In Bas v. Tingy (1800), the Supreme Court held that those statutes had authorized imperfect, or limited, war. In Talbot v. Seeman (1801), a case that involved issues arising from the Quasi-War, Chief Justice John Marshall held that since the “whole powers of war” are “vested in Congress,” it is for that body alone to authorize perfect or imperfect war. In Little v. Barreme (1804), Marshall emphasized that the president, as Commander in Chief, is subject to statutory restriction. One of the statutes passed by Congress in the Quasi-War with France authorized the president to seize vessels that sailed to French ports. But President Adams ordered American ships to capture vessels which sailed to or from French ports, and, in his opinion for the Court, Marshall held that Adams’s order had violated the statute. Subsequent judicial rulings have reiterated that the Commander in Chief may be controlled by statute. Chief Justice Marshall’s opinion was especially significant since it trumpeted the very principle embedded in the appointment by the Continental Congress of George Washington as Commander in Chief of America’s military forces in the American Revolutionary War, to wit: the Commander in Chief is required to obey instructions and directions from Congress. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_140","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_140","title":"President Thomas Jefferson understood limitations in the Commander in Chief Clause. In 1801, in his first annual message to Congress, he reported the arrogant demands made by Joseph Caramanly, the pasha of Tripoli. Unless the United States paid tribute, the pasha threatened to seize American ships and citizens. In response, Jefferson sent a small squadron to the Mediterranean to protect against the threatened attack. He then asked Congress for further guidance, since he was “unauthorized by the Constitution, without the sanction of Congress, to go beyond the line of defense.” It was left to Congress to authorize “measures of offense.” Jefferson’s understanding of the War Clause underwent no revision. Like Jefferson, President James Madison was aggrieved by the punishment and harassment inflicted on United States vessels. In 1812, he expressed to Congress his extreme resentment of the British practices of seizing American ships and seamen and inducing Indian tribes to attack the United States. Madison complained, but said the question of “whether the United States shall remain passive under these progressive usurpations and these accumulating wrongs, or, opposing force, to force in defense of their national rights” is “a solemn question which the Constitution wisely confides to the legislative department of the Government.” ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_141","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_141","title":"The War of 1812 ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_142","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_142","title":"Madison, the leading architect of the War Clause and, indeed, the Constitution, had led the way to vest in Congress, not the president, the authority to initiate hostilities. He remained throughout his career a paragon of consistency on the question of the constitutional repository of authority to commence war or lesser military hostilities. In 1793, Madison, in the context of a debate with Hamilton on issues arising out of the “Genet Controversy,” provided a powerful summation of the Framers’ design of the War Clause. “Every just view that can be taken of this subject,” he wrote, “admonishes the necessity if a rigid adherence to the simple, the received, and the fundamental doctrine of the constitution, that the power to declare war, including the power of judging the causes of war, is fully and exclusively vested in the legislature; that the executive  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_143","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_143","title":"has no right, in any case, to decide the question, whether there is or is not cause for declaring war; that the right of convening and informing congress, whenever such a question seems to call for a decision, is all the right which the constitution has deemed requisite or proper.”   As president, in the War of 1812, Madison deferred to Congress on the question of using military force and declaring war against England. He asserted no unilateral powers and did not push the limits of his authority. His reaction to British outrages reflected what the Framers assumed a president would do in circumstances where the possibility of war would arise: refer the question of a response to the legislative branch. This strict adherence to constitutional forms by not handling the threat until it had reached our back yard, may have been one reason that the redcoats were able to march into Washington and burn the houses of government to the ground. On the other hand, the United States ultimately declared victory in the War of 1812. And so we must ask ourselves, what lessons about the war power can be learned from this experience? ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_149","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_149","title":"President James K. Polk initiated war with Mexico in 1846 when he ordered an army into a disputed area on the Texas-Mexico border, pursuant to his goal of expanding U.S. territory. Polk publicly offered the rationale that Mexico had invaded the United States, which, if true, would justify a response by the Commander in Chief. With that declaration, Polk stampeded Congress into recognizing a state of war with Mexico. But the U.S. House of Representatives, including Rep. Abraham Lincoln, did not believe Polk’s version of events and voted to censure him for “unnecessarily and unconstitutionally” starting the Mexican War. Polk’s act of dissembling before the nation is noteworthy for its demonstration of his interpretation that Congress, alone, could authorize hostilities. Thus, he did not assert a unilateral presidential power to initiate  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_150","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_150","title":"military force. Rather, he attempted to change the fact pattern so that he could rationalize his action within the context of the Commander in Chief Clause. But, a discerning Congress, jealous of its powers and responsibilities, held fast to constitutional principles and halted Polk’s usurpation of the power to make war.  Polk’s unilateral initiation of war against Mexico, was reminiscent, Lincoln wrote, of “the Kingly oppressions” that led the Framers of the Constitution to vest the war power in Congress, rather than the president. Rep. Lincoln famously wrote, in a letter to his law partner in Springfield, Illinois: “Allow the President to invade a neighboring nation, whenever he shall deem it necessary to repel invasion, and you allow him to do so, whenever he may choose to say he deems it necessary for such purpose—and you allow him to make war at his pleasure.… This, our Convention understood to be the most oppressive of all Kingly oppressions; and they resolved to so frame the Constitution that no one man should hold the power of bringing the oppression upon us.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_105","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_105","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si34047","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si34072","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Slide16964","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The Mexican-American War","thumbnail":"dr/Slide16964_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si17049","Image_195","Text_Caption_148","Text_Caption_149","Text_Caption_150","btn_previous_105","si34047","si34072"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide16964"}}},{"id":"si17134","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si17136","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_52","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_52","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_39","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_39","title":"The Civil War ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_151","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_151","title":"Lincoln as Commander in Chief ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_152","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_152","title":"President Abraham Lincoln’s actions in the Civil War have generated questions and controversies, but none of his assertions of power involved a claim to a unilateral power as Commander in Chief to initiate war. In the Prize Cases of 1863, the Supreme Court upheld Lincoln’s blockade against the rebellious Confederacy as a constitutional response to the sudden invasion of the Union, which began with the attack on Fort Sumter. During oral argument, the Lincoln Administration told the Court that Congress, not the president, is vested with the war power. The Court agreed. Justice Robert Grier stated that, as Commander in Chief, the president “has no power to initiate or declare war either against a foreign nation or a domestic state,” but in the event of an invasion, the president was not only authorized “but bound to resist force by force. He does not initiate the war, but is bound to accept the challenge without waiting for the special legislative authority.” The president, the Court reasoned, had no choice but to meet the crisis in the shape it presented itself “without waiting for Congress to baptize it with a name; and no name given to it by him or them could change the fact.” ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_153","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_153","title":"On His Authority as Commander in Chief ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_154","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_154","title":"Apart from Lincoln’s early acts while Congress was in recess—suspension of habeas corpus, calling up the militia, raising an army and navy, and spending funds from the treasury, among them—which we have considered, there remains the need to review his Emancipation Proclamation on January 1, 1863, declaring as Commander in Chief, that all slaves in rebellious states “shall then, thence forward, and forever, be free.” In his final draft, Lincoln made no mention of any statutes; rather, he grounded his act on his authority as Commander in Chief. The proclamation, Lincoln declared was “sincerely to be an act of justice warranted by the Constitution, upon military necessity.” Lincoln’s proclamation stood on a solid legal foundation. Under the International Law of War, every Commander in Chief in any theater of battle has the authority to seize enemy property, provided the seizure serves the ends of war. In Lincoln’s case, it was easy for him to determine that seizure of slave labor would undermine the ability of the Confederacy to wage war. The loss of labor, alone, inflicted a mighty blow on the southern economy, in addition to the availability of tens of thousands of former slaves who could not fight on behalf of the Union. Lincoln was circumspect in addressing the military “necessity” of the Emancipation Proclamation. Indeed, he had previously rejected similar proposals by his generals on grounds that their requests were premature. Second, Lincoln was mindful of the limitations of his authority as Commander in Chief to liberate southern slaves. He did not, for example, attempt to emancipate all slaves, for he did not possess such sweeping authority. His power was limited to freeing slaves in states that were engaged in rebellion against the United States. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_158","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_158","title":"In response to the outbreak of war in Europe in 1914, President Woodrow Wilson issued proclamations of neutrality. American citizens sailing the high seas were at risk after Germany announced that the waters around the British isles constituted a war zone, and warned neutral ships to stay out of those waters. On May 1, 1915, the American tanker, Gulflight, was torpedoed, with the loss of two lives. A few days later, another German torpedo sank the British steamer Lusitania. Of the 1,198 passengers who died in that attack, 124 were American. The Lusitania carried war material, including rifle cartridges, shrapnel, and shell castings.   In response, Wilson prepared the nation for war. In a message to Congress on December 7, 1915, he recommended an increase in the size of the army, acceleration of shipbuilding, and strengthening of the   ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_159","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_159","title":"merchant marine. Despite these recommendations, his reelection campaign of 1916 relied heavily on a promise to keep America out of war. On the eve of his election to a second term, Wilson declared, “I am not expecting this country to get into war.”  On January 31, 1917, the German government announced that on and after February 1, it would use submarines against all ships in designated areas on the high seas. On February 3, Wilson addressed a joint session of Congress. He did not expect the German threat to be executed, but if it should be, he would come before Congress again “to ask that authority be given to me to use any means that may be necessary for the protection of our seamen and our people in the prosecution of their peaceful and legitimate errands on the high seas.” On February 28, Wilson returned to Congress. Two American vessels had been sunk. Since Congress was about to adjourn, he asked for authority to practice “armed neutrality.” Wilson declared: “No doubt I already possess that authority without special warrant of law, by the plain implication of my constitutional duties and power, but I prefer in the present circumstances, not to act upon general implication. I wish to feel that the authority and the power of the Congress are behind me in whatever it may be necessary for me to do.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_111","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_111","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si34521","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si34546","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_112","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_112","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si34600","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si34625","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Slide17260","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The First World War","thumbnail":"dr/Slide17260_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si17350","Text_Caption_157","Text_Caption_158","Text_Caption_159","btn_previous_111","si34521","si34546","btn_next_112","si34600","si34625"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide17260"}}},{"id":"si17515","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si17517","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_56","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_56","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_44","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_44","title":"President Wilson and the Arming of the Merchant Navy ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_162","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_162","title":"Congress Declares War ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_163","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_163","title":"The House passed the bill on March 1 by a vote of 403 to 13, but a filibuster in the Senate prevented a vote on the measure and Congress dissolved on March 4. On March 12, President Wilson announced that he would put armed guards on merchant ships without congressional authority. When he summoned Congress for a special session on April 2, Wilson confessed that his plan had been futile and asked for a declaration of war, saying, “Armed neutrality … is practically certain to draw us into the war without either the rights or the effectiveness of belligerents.” The recent loss of American merchant ships to German submarines, coupled with the disclosure of the “Zimmerman Note,” in which the German Foreign Affairs Minister proposed a German-Mexican alliance, with Mexico getting New Mexico, Arizona, and Texas, fueled the rising war fever. Upon Wilson’s request that Congress declare “the recent course of the German government to be, in fact, nothing less than war,” it declared war.  The arming of merchant vessels without incorporating them into the naval force was of dubious legality at international law. Moreover, an act of Congress of 1819 authorized any merchant vessel of American registry to defend itself against aggression or search by another merchant ship but not against a public armed vessel of a nation in amity with the United States. Further, no act of Congress authorized the president to assign gun crews to private vessels. It seems clear that Wilson’s action was illegal. Yet, to his credit, he never asserted on behalf of the Commander in Chief Clause an executive power to initiate military hostilities or to wage war without congressional authorization. In response to FDR’s request, Congress also declared war on Germany and Italy a few days later.  Prior to the attack on Pearl Harbor, FDR cited national emergency as a basis for actions to aid American allies in their wars against Germany and Italy. In June 1940, when France requested additional assistance from the United States, Roosevelt declared his admiration for the courage of the French in resisting German invaders and pledged continuing assistance of airplanes, artillery, and munitions. When France requested some old U.S. destroyers to increase its naval presence in the Mediterranean, Roosevelt claimed he had no such authority: “Any exchange for American destroyers would probably be unacceptable because of the enormous sea area which must be patrolled by us and would require Congressional action which might be very difficult to get. Our old destroyers cannot be sold as obsolete as is proved by fact. All of them are now in commission and in use or are in process of being commissioned for actual use.” Despite that explanation, some members of Congress heard rumors that American destroyers were being transferred, or that negotiations to transfer them were underway. Aides suggested various ways of transferring the vessels without obtaining authorization from Congress. The administration enlisted the aid of retired General Pershing, who urged that the United States give England and Canada “at least fifty of the over-age destroyers which are left from the days of the World War.” Wendell Wilkie, Republican candidate for the presidency in 1940, assured the administration that he would not make an issue of the transfer in the campaign.  On September 3, President Roosevelt announced an agreement to exchange 50 “over-age’ destroyers to Britain in return for the right to use British bases in the Caribbean and Newfoundland. The executive agreement was struck at a juncture in what could convincingly be seen as a genuine national emergency. The British stood alone against Hitler. The Nazi armies controlled France and were poised for an invasion of England. If Britain fell, Hitler’s conquest of western Europe would be complete and the danger to the United States grave. Of the 100 or so destroyers in English waters, about half had been destroyed or badly damaged when Churchill in May of 1940 asked the United States for a loan of 40 or 50 destroyers to help the Royal Navy hold the English Channel. A month later, as more destroyers were put out of commission, he renewed his request to Roosevelt: “Nothing is so important for us.… We must ask therefore as a matter of life and death to be reinforced with these destroyers.” The British King followed with a personal plea. Churchill, in July, had written: “Mr. President, with great respect I must tell you that in the long history of the world this is a thing to do NOW.” In August, “The worth of destroyer that you can spare to us is measured in rubies.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_117","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_117","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si34995","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35020","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_118","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_118","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35074","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35099","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_717","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_717","roles":{}},{"id":"Image_718","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_718","roles":{}},{"id":"Text_Caption_711","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_711","title":"Wickes-class destroyers similar to those sent to England. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide17648","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"FDR Supports the British","thumbnail":"dr/Slide17648_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si17682","si17684","Text_Caption_167","Text_Caption_168","btn_previous_117","si34995","si35020","btn_next_118","si35074","si35099","Image_717","Image_718","Text_Caption_711"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide17648"}}},{"id":"si17762","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si17764","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_59","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_59","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_47","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_47","title":"The Legality of American Support for the British ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_169","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_169","title":"United States v. Curtiss-Wright ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_170","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_170","title":"Roosevelt had been persuaded by Churchill’s powerful pleas. His own estimate of matters convinced him that the hour was critical and that the United States needed to come to the aid of England. Before acting, he consulted with members of Congress and sought their consent. The question arose: Did the president possess the legal authority to conclude the destroyers-for-bases deal? Attorney General (and a future Supreme Court Justice) Robert H. Jackson advised Roosevelt the he did, indeed, possess the requisite authority to strike the agreement with England. Jackson invoked statutory authority. An act of 1883 authorized the president to sell naval vessels, and the act of 1940 authorized him to transfer, exchange, sell, or otherwise dispose of naval vessels and equipment if the chief of naval operations should certify that the equipment was not essential to the defense of the United States. When the naval chief certified that the destroyers might be spared, the essential criterion for the exchange had been satisfied. But Jackson also invoked the Commander in Chief Clause and the Supreme Court’s opinion in United States v. Curtiss-Wright (1936), for the proposition that the president enjoyed broad, though not unlimited, authority in the conduct of American foreign policy. The Curtiss-Wright case, discussed in detail in a subsequent section, has generated considerable controversy about the scope of presidential power in foreign affairs but, to Jackson’s credit, the opinion was cited as a supportive element of executive authority to conclude the agreement. Roosevelt and Jackson never asserted a presidential prerogative power to take action contrary to a statute. At all events, Roosevelt justified his decision to strike the agreement in the name of national security, explained that “preparation for defense is an inalienable prerogative of a sovereign state.” ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_119","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_119","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35153","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35178","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_120","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_120","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35232","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35257","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_719","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_719","roles":{}},{"id":"Text_Caption_712","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_712","title":"Sir Winston Churchill. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide17728","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The Legality of American Support for the British","thumbnail":"dr/Slide17728_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si17762","si17764","Text_Caption_169","Text_Caption_170","btn_previous_119","si35153","si35178","btn_next_120","si35232","si35257","Image_719","Text_Caption_712"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide17728"}}},{"id":"si17842","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si17844","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_60","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_60","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_48","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_48","title":"Congressional Retroactive Ratification ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_172","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_172","title":"One of the strengths of Jackson’s memo in support of the agreement lay in its discerning quality. While the destroyer exchange was permissible, he emphasized that an act of June 15, 1917, forbade sending out of the United States, vessels built under an agreement to deliver them to a belligerent nation, during a war in which the nation was neutral. This, Jackson said, would preclude delivery to Great Britain of the mosquito boats currently under construction for the United States Navy. Jackson also asserted that the president may accept the naval bases without a treaty if acceptance entailed no legal obligation on the part of the United States. Churchill’s insistence that the bases be considered as gifts, alleviated that legal concern. On September 3, 1940, President Roosevelt announced the exchange.   Roosevelt’s deal met with considerable criticism from contemporary legal experts. Some regarded his exchange of vessels for bases a violation of the United States’ neutral status and a violation of American and international law. Some critics contended that the president had usurped congressional power to define for the United States the requirements of international law respecting neutrality and an invasion of congressional power to declare war. In March 1941, Congress granted its blessing to the president’s action, when it provided retroactive ratification through the enactment of the Lend-Lease Act, which provided express authority for the president to transfer “defense articles” to other countries when he deemed this vital to the defense of the United States. As a consequence, foreign nations became beneficiaries of the act by virtue of their relation to the Axis Powers. In short, Lend-Lease amounted to a declaration of limited war. ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_173","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_173","title":"A Cobelligerent of Great Britain ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_174","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_174","title":"The Lend-Lease Act, as a declaration of limited war, sought to limit presidential action. It provided that “nothing in this Act shall be construed to authorize or permit the authorization of convoying vessels by vessels of the United States.” In April 1941, however, the United States took over the patrol of the Atlantic to the 25th parallel, releasing British ships for other duties. The line was subsequently adjusted to include Iceland on July 7, 1941, when the president informed Congress that by agreement with the government of Iceland he had as Commander in Chief landed marines to supplement and eventually replace British forces there. In the same message he announced that he had instituted convoys to Iceland and all other “strategic outposts.” On September 11, he announced that he had ordered the Navy to attack Axis submarines on sight. On November 17, at President Roosevelt’s request, Congress authorized the arming of merchant vessels. On December 11, Germany and Italy declared war on the United States, which responded with general declarations of war on the same day. Roosevelt committed an act of war when he established a naval patrol for some hundreds of miles into the Atlantic to report the presence of German and, later, Italian vessels. The information was relayed to the British. This made the United States, to a limited extent, a cobelligerent of Great Britain. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide17888","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The Lend-Lease Act","thumbnail":"dr/Slide17888_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si17922","si17924","Text_Caption_173","Text_Caption_174","btn_previous_123","si35469","si35494","btn_next_124","si35548","si35573","Image_722","Text_Caption_717"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide17888"}}},{"id":"si18002","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si18004","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_62","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_62","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_50","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_50","title":"War Powers Resolution (1973) ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_175","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_175","title":"An Effort to Curb Executive War Actions ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_176","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_176","title":"Concerned that its role in making decisions to send military forces into combat had atrophied, Congress attempted to reassert itself in 1973 with the passage of the War Powers Resolution. The statute sought to curb presidential war making and to require the “collective judgment” of the president and Congress before the U.S. troops could be committed to hostilities. The resolution was passed within the immediate context of the white-hot debate surrounding American involvement in the Vietnam War, but it can also be set within the larger context of legislative efforts to recapture the war power, which had been usurped by presidents since Harry S. Truman’s unilateral entry into the Korean War.  President Richard M. Nixon vetoed the War Powers Resolution. In his veto message, he stated that it was unwise, impractical, and dangerous to legislate the procedure by which the president and Congress would share the war power. Moreover, he argued that the measure infringed on the president’s constitutional power as Commander in Chief. Nixon informed Congress the “only way in which the constitutional powers of a branch of the Government can be altered is by amending the Constitution—and any attempt to make such alterations by legislation alone is clearly without force.” But both houses of Congress summoned the requisite two-thirds majority to override the veto: the House by a narrow vote of 284 to135 and the Senate by the wider margin of 75 to 18. The resolution thus became law on November 7, 1973. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide17968","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"War Powers Resolution (1973)","thumbnail":"dr/Slide17968_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si18002","si18004","Text_Caption_175","Text_Caption_176","btn_previous_125","si35627","si35652","btn_next_126","si35706","si35731","Image_723","Text_Caption_718"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide17968"}}},{"id":"si18082","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si18084","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_63","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_63","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_51","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_51","title":"Procedures Governing the Deployment of U.S. Forces ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_178","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_178","title":"The War Powers Resolution consists of three main procedures to govern the deployment of U.S. military force abroad:   presidential consultation with Congress; presidential reports to Congress; and  congressional termination of military action.   The purpose of the resolution, according to section 2(a), is “to insure that the collective judgment” of the president and Congress will apply to both the introduction of American troops into hostilities and the length of their involvement. Under section 3 of the resolution, the president is required “in every possible instance” to consult with Congress before he introduces troops into “hostilities” or into “situations where imminent involvement in hostilities is clearly indicated by the circumstances.” This provision obviously vests the president with discretion to determine, at a minimum, whether consultation is “possible” and, perhaps somewhat more generously, whether it is desirable. Apparently, it also permits the president to determine when, how, and even whom to consult. Debate has arisen about the very meaning of “consultation.” Presidents have tended to treat it as a synonym for notification, while Congress has viewed it as a joint deliberation on a pending issue or problem.  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_127","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_127","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35785","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35810","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_128","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_128","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35864","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si35889","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_724","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_724","roles":{}},{"id":"Image_725","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_725","roles":{}},{"id":"Text_Caption_719","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_719","title":"American troops approaching Omaha Beach on D-Day. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide18048","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Procedures Governing the Deployment of U.S. Forces","thumbnail":"dr/Slide18048_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si18082","si18084","Text_Caption_178","btn_previous_127","si35785","si35810","btn_next_128","si35864","si35889","Image_724","Image_725","Text_Caption_719"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide18048"}}},{"id":"si18162","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si18164","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_64","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_64","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_52","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_52","title":"The War Clause vs. The War Powers Resolution ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_179","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_179","title":"The Balance of Power between the Branches ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_180","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_180","title":"The War Powers Resolution is commonly thought to have been aimed at limiting presidential power by re-establishing congressional primacy in a process that had evolved away from them. But the actual effect of the act was, ironically, to empower the president to take action that the Constitution says he may not otherwise take. The War Clause of the Constitution (Article I, Section 8) requires a majority vote of both houses of Congress to authorize the initiation of hostilities against a foreign nation. It follows, therefore, that no smaller contingent in Congress may lay claim to the war power. And section 3 of the resolution contains a greater, more fundamental, constitutional flaw. Since the provision empowers the president to introduce troops into combat without prior congressional authorization, it not only repudiates the resolution’s stated aim of ensuring the “collective judgment” of both branches but also grants the president more power than he derives from the Constitution. The delegation doctrine prohibits Congress from delegating, sharing or transferring its constitutional powers to the president. Thus, Congress may not delegate to the executive its lawmaking or appropriations powers; nor may it delegate its sole and exclusive authority to decide for war.   The grant of authority to the president to introduce troops into hostilities also contradicts section 8(d) of the resolution, which states that the resolution is not intended to alter the constitutional authority of the president or Congress or to grant any authority to the president that “he would not have had in the absence” of the statute. Any attempt to vest the war power in the president, properly undertaken, would require a constitutional amendment since it involves a fundamental transfer of power from the legislature to the executive. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide18128","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The War Clause vs. The War Powers Resolution","thumbnail":"dr/Slide18128_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si18162","si18164","Text_Caption_179","Text_Caption_180","btn_previous_129","si35943","si35968","btn_next_130","si36022","si36047","Image_726","Image_727","Text_Caption_720"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide18128"}}},{"id":"si18329","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si18331","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_66","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_66","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_54","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_54","title":"Restraints on Presidential War Powers ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_184","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_184","title":"Reporting Requirements ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_185","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_185","title":"Section 4 of the resolution imposes on the president important reporting requirements, which establish the foundation of congressional control over war making. When the president introduces forces into hostilities or into situations in which hostilities are imminent, he is required to submit a report to Congress within 48 hours. The submission of the report triggers the mechanisms of congressional control, but these are thwarted if the report is delayed. Section 5(b) provides that after a report “is submitted or is required to be submitted,” the president must terminate the use of U.S. forces after 60 days unless Congress has declared war, authorized the action, or is physically unable to convene as a result of an attack on the United States. The president may extend the 60-day period for an additional 30 days if he certifies that it is necessary to protect American forces and remove them from conflict.   Section 5 vests in Congress two mechanisms for controlling the involvement of U.S. forces in hostilities: Congress can decide to withhold authorization of presidential action during the 60-to-90 day period or it can pass a concurrent resolution at any time to order the president to remove forces involved in hostilities. The clock has been triggered on only two occasions. In 1975, President Gerald Ford reported to Congress after the Mayaguez had been captured, but the issue of the time limit was moot because the military operation had been completed by the time the report was filed. In the second instance, Congress itself started the clock in October 1983 with the passage of the Lebanon Resolution, which reflected its frustration with Ronald Reagan’s unwillingness to file a report under section 4(a)(1) after Marines he had sent to Lebanon became targets of hostile fire in August 1983. That measure permitted President Reagan to keep forces in Lebanon for a period of eighteen months, but he removed most of the troops in February 1984.      The resolution’s most immediate and powerful congressional mechanism to control war making—the passage of a concurrent resolution to order the removal of troops engaged in hostilities—was gutted by the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. Chadha (1983), which held the legislative veto unconstitutional. Although the case involved the use of a one-house legislative veto, the decision cast doubt on the legality of any legislative veto that was not presented to the president for his signature. President Obama’s assertions represented an about-face from the position he had articulated as a candidate for the presidency. In his presidential campaign in 2007, Senator Obama was asked by Charlie Savage, then a reporter for the Boston Globe (now a reporter for The New York Times), under what circumstances a president would possess the constitutional authority to order strategic bombing of suspected nuclear sites in Iran without first receiving authorization from Congress. History, he said, has demonstrated that military action “is most successful when it is authorized and supported by the Legislative branch.
