","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Image_803","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_803","roles":{}},{"id":"Slide19808","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"No Authorization Required?","thumbnail":"dr/Slide19808_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si81296","Image_138","btn_previous_149","si37523","si37548","btn_next_150","si37602","si37627","Text_Caption_788","Image_803"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide19808"}}},{"id":"si19929","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si19931","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_76","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_76","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_64","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_64","title":"Congressionally Authorized vs. Presidential Wars ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_211","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_211","title":"We Go to Eleven ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_212","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_212","title":"Congress, as we have seen, enjoys by virtue of the War Clause, the sole and exclusive authority to initiate military hostilities on behalf of the American people. At its discretion, Congress may institute a state of war by choosing to either formally “declare” war on enemies of the United States, or by authorizing hostilities through passage of a joint resolution. There are also two kinds of war, which American courts have called “perfect,” or general, and “imperfect,” or limited, wars. In 1782 the Federal Court of Appeals, the prize court established by the Continental Congress, explained the difference. It stated: “The writers upon the law of nations, speaking of different kinds of war, distinguish them into perfect and imperfect: A perfect war is that which destroys the national peace and tranquility, and lays the foundation of every act of hostility. The imperfect war is that which does not entirely destroy the public tranquility, but interrupts it only in some particulars, as in the case of reprisals.” At all events, the president has no constitutional authority to initiate war or lesser military hostilities, but that has not proven to be an impediment to executive war making. Indeed, presidential aggrandizement of the war power has become a common theme for the past 60 years.   It is baffling that scholars, commentators, and politicians have so often said of American history that a declaration of solemn war, fully invoking international law, has been issued on only five occasions. In fact, Congress has passed resolutions declaring general war, declarations pure and simple, on 11 occasions (see right). All of these resolutions authorized the president to use the army, navy, and the militia for the prosecution of war. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Image_139","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_139","roles":{}},{"id":"btn_previous_151","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_151","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si37681","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si37706","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_152","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_152","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si37760","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si37785","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Slide19895","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Congressionally Authorized vs. Presidential Wars","thumbnail":"dr/Slide19895_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si19929","si19931","Text_Caption_211","Text_Caption_212","Image_139","btn_previous_151","si37681","si37706","btn_next_152","si37760","si37785"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide19895"}}},{"id":"si20066","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_77","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_77","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_66","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_66","title":"Conditional Declarations of War ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_215","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_215","title":"Buchanan and McKinley ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_216","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_216","title":"In addition, Congress has passed four conditional declarations of war. These are congressional “ultimatums,” instances in which Congress asserts demands and directs the president to act, if they are not met. Two episodes are illustrative. In 1853, an American naval vessel was on the Paraguayan side of waters allegedly common to Paraguay and Argentina. Paraguay had closed its waters to foreign ships of war, and a Paraguayan fort shelled the Water Witch, which returned fire. In his annual message to Congress on December 8, 1857, President James Buchanan announced his intention to demand redress and asked Congress to authorize him to use “other means in the event of a refusal.” By joint resolution of June 2, 1858, he was authorized to “adopt such measures and use such force as, in his judgment, may be necessary and advisable.” Several ships of war were sent out and the difficulties with Paraguay were adjusted by commissioners, without resorting to violence. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_217","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_217","title":"A second, illustrative episode, occurred during the administration of President William McKinley. On April 20, 1898, President McKinley signed a joint resolution demanding that Spain withdraw from Cuba and relinquish her authority over the island. Congress directed McKinley to use the land and naval forces to implement the resolution. On April 21, Spain severed diplomatic relations with the United States. On April 25, Congress passed an act declaring that a state of war with Spain had existed since April 21.  On several occasions, Congress has authorized limited war, that is, action restricted as to objective or as to instrument. The Supreme Court has held that the exercise of authority to determine the size, scope, and direction of war, is part and parcel of the congressional war power, described by James Madison as authority to “commence, conclude and continue war.” These acts, harking back to the earliest days of the republic, reveal presidential deference to Congress.  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_153","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_153","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si37839","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si37864","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_154","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_154","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si37918","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si37943","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Slide19981","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Conditional Declarations of War","thumbnail":"dr/Slide19981_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si20066","Text_Caption_215","Text_Caption_216","Text_Caption_217","btn_previous_153","si37839","si37864","btn_next_154","si37918","si37943"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide19981"}}},{"id":"si20151","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si20153","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_78","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_78","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_67","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_67","title":"The Barbary States ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_218","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_218","title":"Selling Treaties of Peace ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_219","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_219","title":"The United States has had a long, difficult history with the Barbary states, and their predations against American shipping. The four Barbary states—Morocco, Tripoli, Algiers, and Tunis—considered themselves at war with all Christian states with which they did not have treaties of peace. They sold treaties of peace and preyed upon the shipping of all states with which they were at war. The larger European powers easily could have destroyed them, but they found it more profitable to pay the Barbary states and keep them in business, for they greatly reduced the competition of smaller powers, such as the Italian states. In 1783, Benjamin Franklin wrote that “it was said to be a maxim among English merchants that ‘if there were no Algiers, it would be worth England’s while to build one.’”        The Continental Congress ratified a treaty of peace with Morocco in 1787 and with Tunis in 1798. Relations with Morocco were relatively peaceful and friendly until 1802, when the emperor denounced the peace treaty and declared war. On November 4, 1803, President Thomas Jefferson informed Congress that a Moroccan cruiser had seized an American merchant vessel, but the United States frigate Philadelphia had then captured both. Jefferson asked Congress “to consider the provisional authorities which may be necessary to restrain the depredations of this power should they be continued.” On December 5, he informed Congress that the emperor of Morocco had disavowed the action of his cruiser, had agreed to amicable adjustment of all difficulties, and had confirmed the treaty of 1787. In 1785, Algerian corsairs seized two American vessels and enslaved their crews; in 1793, they captured several American vessels. On December 16, 1793, President George Washington informed Congress of his efforts “for the ransom of our citizens and establishment of peace with Algiers.” But on March 3, 1794, he reported lack of progress in the negotiations with Algiers. On March 27, 1794, Congress passed an act reciting that “the depredations committed by the Algerine corsairs on the commerce of the United States render it necessary that a naval force be provided for its protection,” and the act authorized the construction of six frigates. A treaty of peace with Algiers was ratified by the Senate on March 2, 1796. On March 15, 1796, Washington informed Congress that peace had been made but suggested that Congress might not wish to discontinue all naval construction. Congress, on April 20, voted that three frigates should be completed.  Congressional authorization of hostilities against the Barbary states is illustrative of early governmental adherence to the provisions of the Constitution, but the limited war with France from 1798 to 1801 is perhaps more familiar to students as an exercise of the sweeping authority of Congress to impose statutory limits on the Commander in Chief in the conduct of war. The war, moreover, gave rise to a series of landmark Supreme Court decisions that reaffirmed the Framers’ design of the War Clause and remain good law. Yet, as we shall see, advocates of unilateral presidential power on matters of war and peace have curiously advanced the Quasi-War as precedent for an executive war-making authority despite the fact that President John Adams asserted no unilateral authority to initiate hostilities. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_225","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_225","title":"As a result of French interference with American shipping, President Adams asked Congress to prepare the nation for war. While he promised to pursue negotiations toward peaceful resolution, political conditions abroad compelled him to recommend to Congress “effectual measures of defense.” Congress debated various bills and, in the end, enacted two dozen statutes authorizing Adams to engage in military acts against France. Congress suspended commercial trade with France, terminated the 1778 treaty of amity and commerce with France, established a Department of Navy and a Marine Corps, augmented the navy, provided for raising an army in the case of need, reinforced the defense of ports and harbors, and funded additional cannons, arms, and ammunition. It also passed an act that authorized the president to instruct commanders of United States naval vessels to seize as prize any armed vessel that had, or which might be found hovering around the U.S. coast, for the purpose of committing depredations on vessels belonging to U.S. citizens. Another act , pursuant to congressional authority to grant letters of marque and reprisals, authorized the president to grant commissions to owners of private armed ships to capture armed French vessels and to recapture American vessels. Another statute provided that if the president should find American seamen impressed on the ships of the enemies of France captured by the French were maltreated, he must cause “the most rigorous retaliation” to be inflicted on French prisoners. The naval war founded on these acts was ended by a treaty in 1801. Attorney General Charles Lee, after reviewing the laws that Congress had enacted, advised President Adams that “there exists not only an actual maritime war between France and the United States, but a maritime war authorized by both nations.” For his part, Adams wrote Secretary of State John Marshall on September 4, 1800, to say that he believed that Congress “had declared war within the meaning of the Constitution against that republic, under certain restrictions and limitations.”   Alexander Hamilton clearly understood the limits of presidential power in the Quasi-War. Congress passed legislation on May 28, 1798, authorizing the president to seize armed French vessels. He was asked what American ship commanders could do prior to the enactment of that bill. Hamilton was “not ready to say that [the President] has any other power than merely to employ” ships with authority “to repel force by force (but not to capture) and to repress hostilities within our waters including a marine league from our coasts.” Any actions beyond those measures “must fall under the idea of reprisals & requires the sanction of that Department which is to declare or make war.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_161","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_161","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si38471","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si38496","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_162","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_162","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si38550","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si38575","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_735","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_735","roles":{}},{"id":"Image_736","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_736","roles":{}},{"id":"Slide20430","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"A War by Any Other Name","thumbnail":"dr/Slide20430_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si20464","si20466","Text_Caption_227","btn_previous_161","si38471","si38496","btn_next_162","si38550","si38575","Image_735","Image_736"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide20430"}}},{"id":"si20544","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si20546","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_83","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_83","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_72","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_72","title":"A Perfect or Imperfect War? ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_228","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_228","title":"Bas v. Tingy and Talbot v. Seeman ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_229","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_229","title":"The Quasi-War raised the questions of whether Congress might declare an imperfect war, or only a perfect war, and whether the determination of the scope of imperfect war lay with Congress. The Supreme Court rendered three illuminating decisions, clarifying the authority of Congress over war and the deployment of military force. In the first case, Bas v. Tingy (1800), Justice Samuel Chase noted: “Congress is empowered to declare a general war, or Congress may wage a limited war; limited in place, in objects, and in time. . . . Congress has authorized hostilities on the high seas by certain persons in certain cases. There is no authority given to commit hostilities on land.” In the second case, Talbot v. Seeman (1801), Chief Justice Marshall wrote for the Court: “The whole powers of war being, by the Constitution of the United States, vested in Congress, the acts of that body alone can be resorted to as our guides in this inquiry.” These critical decisions thus recognized that Congress enjoys the sole and exclusive authority to decide whether to initiate war, to determine the size, scope, and nature of the hostilities, and whether the war might be limited to land or sea.  In authorizing war, Congress may place limits on what the president—as Commander in Chief—may and may not do. An act passed by Congress on February 9, 1799, authorized the president to instruct commanders of ships to seize vessels sailing “to” French ports. President Adams exceeded the statute by issuing an order directing American ships to capture vessels sailing “to” or “from” French ports. Captain George Little followed Adams’ order by seizing a Danish ship sailing from a French port. He was sued for damages, and the case came to the Supreme Court. Barreme (1804) ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_231","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_231","title":"Chief Justice Marshall, writing for the Court in Little v. Barreme (1804), admitted that the case gave him much difficulty. He confessed that the first “bias” of his mind was very strongly in favor of the opinion that although the instructions from President Adams “could not give a right, they might yet excuse [a military officer] from damages.” Initially, Marshall assumed an “implicit obedience, which military men usually pay to the orders of their superiors, which indeed is indispensably necessary to every military system.” To Marshall, that system of military hierarchy seemed to justify the actions of Captain Little, “who is placed by the laws of his country in a situation which in general requires that he should obey them.” And yet on further reflection, Marshall decided that Little could be sued for damages: “I have been convinced that I was mistaken, and I have receded from this first opinion. I  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_232","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_232","title":"cannot change the nature of the transaction, or legalize an act which, without those instructions, would have been a plain trespass.” Since the president’s instructions collided with the act of Congress, they were illegal and could neither justify the seizure nor excuse Captain Little from damages.   At bottom, the Supreme Court’s rulings reminded the nation that presidential orders, including those issued as Commander in Chief, are subject to restrictions imposed by Congress. In a tradition that dates back to the creation of the Commander in Chief title in England in 1639, a Commander in Chief is accountable to and subordinate to a political superior. In the conduct of war, the president is subordinate to and accountable to Congress. He is duty-bound to obey the instructions and directions of Congress. ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_169","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_169","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39103","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39128","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_170","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_170","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39182","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39207","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_682","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_682","title":"Pacificus ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_683","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_683","title":"When war broke out between Great Britain and France, President Washington issued his Proclamation of Neutrality because he feared that military initiatives by private citizens might embroil the United States in that European war. Such private actions would undermine the war powers of Congress. Secretary of State Thomas Jefferson noted in private correspondence: “if one citizen has a right to go to war of his own authority, every citizen has the same. If every citizen has that right, then the nation (which is composed of all of its citizens) has a right to go to war, by the authority of its individual citizen. But this is not true either on the general principles of society, or by our Constitution, which gives that power to Congress alone.”  President Washington’s Proclamation of Neutrality raised a fundamental issue: did it encroach on the power of  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_684","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_684","title":"Congress to decide on matters of war and peace? Washington, ever solicitous of his constitutional responsibilities, asked his Cabinet of he should call Congress back in session to address the issue, but they advised against it. He was concerned that the United States might be obligated to defend the France by virtue of our 1778 mutual defense treaty. Secretary of Treasury, Alexander Hamilton, responded to the legal attacks on the proclamation. Writing as “Pacificus,” Hamilton argued in what essentially was a series of newspaper op-ed pieces, that the management of foreign affairs was vested in the president, who is “the organ of intercourse between the nation and foreign nations [and] the interpreter of the national treaties.” To say that the proclamation represented the enactment of a new law was “entirely erroneous,” Hamilton argued. It announced only a fact and informed citizens what was required of them under settled laws. Hamilton’s position was modest: the president could proclaim neutrality to keep the United States out of war until Congress has determined to move the country from a state of peace to a state of war. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide20777","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The Proclamation of Neutrality","thumbnail":"dr/Slide20777_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si75194","btn_previous_169","si39103","si39128","btn_next_170","si39182","si39207","Text_Caption_682","Text_Caption_683","Text_Caption_684"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide20777"}}},{"id":"si20891","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si20893","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_87","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_87","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_76","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_76","title":"Madison Rebukes Hamilton ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_238","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_238","title":"Helvidius ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_239","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_239","title":"In a series of responses under the pseudonym, “Helvidius,” James Madison rebuked Hamilton for borrowing a theory of government based on the royal prerogatives of the English crown. Under Hamilton’s interpretation of executive power, Madison declared, “no citizen could any longer guess at the character of the government under which he lives.” Madison was alarmed by Hamilton’s view of presidential power:  “In no part of the constitution is more wisdom to be found, than in the clause which confides the question of war and peace to the legislature, and not to the executive department. . . . War is in fact the true nurse of executive aggrandizement. In war a physical force is to be created; and it is the executive will, which is to direct it. In war, the public treasures are to be unlocked; and it is the executive hand which is to dispense them. In war, the honours and emoluments of office are to be multiplied; and it is the executive patronage under which they are to be enjoyed. It is in war, finally, that laurels are to be gathered; and it is the executive brow they are to encircle. . .” In a prescient observation, Madison stated: “Hence it has grown into an axiom that the executive is the department power most distinguished by its propensity to war; hence it is the practice of all states, in proportion as they are free, to disarm this propensity of its influence.”   ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_171","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_171","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39261","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39286","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_172","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_172","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39340","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39365","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_742","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_742","roles":{}},{"id":"Slide20857","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Madison Rebukes Hamilton","thumbnail":"dr/Slide20857_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si20891","si20893","Text_Caption_238","Text_Caption_239","btn_previous_171","si39261","si39286","btn_next_172","si39340","si39365","Image_742"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide20857"}}},{"id":"si21058","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si21060","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_89","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_89","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_78","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_78","title":"United States v. Smith (1806) ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_242","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_242","title":"Restricting Presidential Action ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_243","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_243","title":"United States v. Smith (1806), involved one of the most significant cases illuminating the power of Congress to restrict presidential action. President Jefferson recognized the importance of enforcement of the Neutrality Act. There was, he believed, extreme danger in allowing private citizens to decide by themselves to deploy armed forces. In his fourth annual message he referred to complaints that people residing in the United States had used armed merchant vessels in defiance of the laws of the other countries: “That individuals should undertake to wage private war, independently of the authority of their country, can not be permitted in a well-ordered society.” The tendency, he said, was “to produce aggression on the laws and rights of other nations and to endanger the peace of our own.”  Numerous cases involving violation of the Neutrality Act came before the federal courts. In 1806, a circuit court reviewed the indictment of Colonel William S. Smith for engaging in military actions against Spain. He claimed that his military expertise “was begun, prepared, and set on foot with the knowledge and approbation of the executive department of our government.” The Court rejected his claim that a president or his assistants could somehow authorize military adventures that violated congressional policy. U.S. Supreme Court Justice, William Paterson, who had been a central figure as a New Jersey delegate to the Constitutional Convention, was riding circuit, and wrote for the Court that the Neutrality Act is “declaratory of the law of nations; and besides, every species of private and unauthorized hostilities is inconsistent with the principles of the social compact, and the very nature, scope, and end of civil government.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_175","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_175","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39577","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39602","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_176","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_176","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39656","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si39681","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_743","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_743","roles":{}},{"id":"Text_Caption_730","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_730","title":"William Paterson. Truman deployed American troops into the Korean War on the assertion of a unilateral executive authority as Commander in Chief. Truman thus became the first president to lay claim to an independent war-making authority, in disregard of our constitutional architecture—the debates in the Constitutional Convention, the various debates in the state ratifying conventions, the Federalist Papers, and other commentaries on the War Clause contemporaneous with the framing of the Constitution, early Supreme Court decisions, and early practice. His assertions became the first in a steady stream of presidential wars, up to and including President Obama, with President Dwight D. Eisenhower as the lone exception to the crowd of presidents seeking to revise the meaning of the War Clause. Truman’s action soon became a precedent for every subsequent occupant of the White House to invoke as justification for their unilateral use of force. Truman’s claims inspired a variety of legal and constitutional theories to rationalize executive war making. The Truman Administration’s novel (at the time) legal argument, generated by his Secretary of State, Dean Acheson, a distinguished senior lawyer at Washington’s leading law firm for many years, who had clerked for Justice Louis Brandeis and still a daily walking companion of Justice Felix Frankfurter, declared that “the President, as Commander in Chief of the Armed Forces of the United States, has full control over the use thereof,” that there was a “traditional power of the President to use the armed forces of the united States without consulting Congress,” and that this had often been done in “the broad interests of American foreign policy.” As the days rolled on, the legal rationale for Truman’s actions grew in width and breadth. The State Department produced a ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_255","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_255","title":"memorandum that purported to list 87 instances in which presidents had sent American forces into combat on their own initiative. Here was the argument based on historical precedent, which would come to cast a large shadow over the debate on the constitutional repository of the war power. Truman found support in the House and the Senate, as well. Senator Paul H. Douglas, a widely respected liberal Democrat from Illinois, sought to bolster Truman’s case. Douglas contended that the president required authority to act swiftly in the face of emergencies. “With tanks, airplanes and the atom bomb, war can become instantaneous and disaster can occur while Congress is assembling and debating.” Though Congress had the power to declare general war, situations “calling for the retail use of force,” Douglas suggested, ought to be left to the to the president, as they had been left throughout American history. There might be “grave dangers” in thus giving the presidency discretionary power to commit troops to battle, but Douglas found consolation in “the sobering and terrible responsibilities of the Office of President itself” and in the fact that action grossly offensive to the national interest and public will could lead to impeachment. Truman, subsequently, would invoke as authority a U.N. Security Council Resolution. On June 26, President Truman announced to the American public that he had conferred with his top foreign policy and defense advisers “about the situation in the Far East created by unprovoked aggression against the Republic of Korea.” He reported that the U.N. Security Council had acted to order a withdrawal of the invading forces to positions north of the 38th parallel and that “in accordance with the resolution of the Security Council, the United States will vigorously support the effort of the Council to terminate this serious breach of the peace.”   On the following day, June 27, Truman announced that North Korea had failed to cease hostilities and withdraw to the 38th parallel. He explained the U.N. process: “The Security council called upon all members of the United Nations to render every assistance to the United Nations in the execution of this resolution. In these circumstances I have ordered United States air and sea forces to give the [South] Korean Government cover and support.” In fact, Truman had already committed American forces prior to the UN call for assistance. As such, Truman had rested his case for unilateral executive war-making authority on the Commander in Chief Clause. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide21487","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Turning Points","thumbnail":"dr/Slide21487_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si21521","si21523","Text_Caption_256","Text_Caption_257","btn_previous_185","si40367","si40392","btn_next_186","si40446","si40471","Image_746","Text_Caption_732"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide21487"}}},{"id":"si21601","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si21603","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_95","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_95","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_85","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_85","title":"The UN Participation Act (1945) ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_259","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_259","title":"Truman’s claim that he had acted under color of authorization from the UN Security Council was false. Still, there remained the question of why he had not followed the UN Participation Act of 1945, enacted by Congress to prevent a president from taking the United States into war without congressional authorization. The Act was passed to clarify the way in which the United States would answer a request from the UN to member states to contribute military forces to a UN action. Article 43 of the UN Charter provides that all UN members shall make available to the Security Council—in accordance with “special agreements”—armed forces and other assistance. The agreements would spell out the numbers and types of forces, their degree of readiness and general location, and the nature of the facilities and assistance to be provided. It was anticipated that each nation would ratify these agreements “in accordance with their respective constitutional processes.” Korea. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide21834","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"With the Consent of Congress","thumbnail":"dr/Slide21834_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si21868","si21917","Image_143","Text_Caption_267","Text_Caption_268","btn_previous_189","si40683","si40708","btn_next_190","si40762","si40787","Image_348","Text_Caption_511","Image_349","Text_Caption_512","Image_350","Image_353","Button_369","si57660","si57692","Button_370","si57748","si57774","Image_747","Text_Caption_733"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide21834"}}},{"id":"si21955","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si21957","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_98","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_98","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_90","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_90","title":"The UN Participation Act ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_269","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_269","title":"Vested in Congress ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_270","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_270","title":"President Truman’s legal rationales for his unilateral deployment of U.S. forces to Korea cannot withstand scrutiny. As we have seen, there is nothing in the origin and development of the Commander in Chief Clause that would sustain Truman’s invocation of it as authority for the president to take the nation to war. Nor is there any substance to the claim that the UN Security Council—or any international organization, for that matter—could authorize the United States to go to war, for at least two reasons. First, the U.S. Constitution vests the war power in both houses of Congress. Accordingly, the treaty power in the U.S.—the president and the Senate—cannot commit the nation to war without the approval of the House of Representatives. Moreover, mere membership in the UN, achieved through the treaty process, does not confer on the UN or the Security Council authority to require the United States to contribute forces to a UN action. The decision to commit forces to a UN mission must be conducted in accord with a member’s “constitutional processes,” leaving to a member state complete discretion to decide whether to participate in the mission. And, it should be noted, the UN is not a substitute for congressional representation of the American people. The decision for American participation in war cannot rest on the will of Lithuania or Italy. Surely, the question of going to war cannot depend on the shifting membership in that body. arguments were advanced to defend President Truman’s entry into the Korean War. At a June 29, 1950, news conference, he was asked if the United States was at war. He replied: “We are not at war.” A reporter asked whether it might be more correct to call the military conflict “a police action under the United Nations.” Truman agreed: “That is exactly what it amounts to.” The characterization of the Korean War as a “police action,” an effort to distract a nation weary from war, did not alter the legal status of Truman’s action. Then, too, the United Nations had rolled into action and trumpeted its calls for member states to provide forces to support the Security Council’s resolution. As the war began to go badly, however, some conservative congressmen realized the implications of unilateral presidential war making for the United States and the Constitution. One of the critics, Rep. Frederic R. Coudert, Jr. (R-NY), observed, “how devastating the precedent they have set in remaining silent while the President took over the powers specifically reserved for Congress in the Constitution.” Truman’s aggrandizement of power, coupled with his intention that he would be sending four more divisions to reinforce the American army in Europe, led Coudert to introduce on January 3, 1951, a resolution declaring it the sense of Congress that ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_277","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_277","title":"“no additional military forces” could be sent abroad “without the prior authorization of the Congress in each instance.” The resolution also provided that no funds appropriated for the armed forces could be used for sending troops abroad. Clearly, Rep. Coudert meant to tie the hands of the president and restore congressional control over troop deployments and the use of military force.  President Truman told a press conference the next day that he did not require the approval of Congress to send more troops to Europe. A week later, he extended his claim of authority: “under the President’s constitutional powers as Commander in Chief of the Armed Forces,” he possessed authority to send troops anywhere in the world, that this power had been “repeatedly recognized by Congress and the courts,” and that his administration would “continue to send troops whenever it is necessary to uphold” its obligations to the United Nations. When asked about constitutional precedents, he said, “I haven’t got it with me just now, but you will find decisions by at least three Chief Justices on that very subject.” Truman said that the opinion of the Court did not require him to consult with Congress, and added, “I do not have to unless I want to. But of course I am polite, and I usually always consult them. . . . The opinions are all in favor of the President’s exercise of the Presidential power when in his judgment it is necessary.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_195","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_195","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41157","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41182","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_196","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_196","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41236","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41261","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Slide22167","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Attempts to Limit Presidential Use of Military Force","thumbnail":"dr/Slide22167_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si22201","Text_Caption_275","Text_Caption_276","Text_Caption_277","btn_previous_195","si41157","si41182","btn_next_196","si41236","si41261"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide22167"}}},{"id":"si22288","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si22290","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_102","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_102","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_94","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_94","title":"Battle is Joined ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_279","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_279","title":"The battle was joined. For the first time in American history, the presidency and Congress were engaged in a “Great Debate” about which branch possessed the authority to commit troops abroad. Senator Robert Taft (R-Ohio), the son of William Howard Taft, the only person ever to have served both as president and Supreme Court Justice (in his case, Chief Justice), rebuked Truman for his usurpation of the war power. The issue of troop deployment, whether to Korea, or to UN missions, or to Europe, was an issue to be debated and decided by Congress. Various senators— Paul Douglas, J.W. Fulbright, Herbert Lehman, and Wayne Morse, among others—challenged Taft. Fulbright declared that if the president thought the defense of America required the dispatch of troops to Europe, “he has the power and the duty to do so.” Senator Fulbright inserted in the Congressional Record the full text of the Supreme Court’s controversial opinion in United States v. Curtiss-Wright (1936), to vindicate the “discretionary power which I believe is inherent in the President of the United States in the field of foreign affairs.” Senator Tom Connally, Chairman of the Senate Committee on Foreign Relations, stated: “The authority of the President as Commander in Chief to send the Armed Forces to any place required by the security interests of the United States has often been questioned, but never denied by authoritative opinion.” Secretary Acheson contended that the president’s power was not only inherent, but exclusive: “Not only has the President the authority to use the Armed Forces in carrying out the broad foreign policy of the United States and implementing treaties, but it is equally clear that this authority may not be interfered with by Congress in the exercise of powers which it has under the Constitution.” And then, in a fit of frustration, he dismissed the relevance of the law and the Constitution: “We are in a position in the world today where the argument as to who has the power to do this, that, or the other thing, is not exactly what is called for from America in this very critical hour.” Authority ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_281","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_281","title":"Distinguished scholars, many of them supportive of President Truman’s broad claims of authority, joined the argument. Henry Steele Commager wrote, “Whatever may be said of the expediency of the Taft-Coudert program, this at least can be said of the principles involved—that they have no support in law or history.” Arthur Schlesinger, Jr., said Taft’s arguments were “demonstrably irresponsible.” But Edward S. Corwin for many years the dean of constitutional scholars, denounced the eminent historians, describing them as “high-flying prerogative men” who ascribed to the president “ a truly royal prerogative in the field of foreign relations . . . without indicating any correlative legal or constitutional control to which he is answerable.” Professor Corwin contended that the fact that Congress had not exercised its power to deploy troops did not mean that it lacked such authority. Rather, in the teaching of Chief Justice Marshall’s opinion in Little v. Barreme, the president was obligated to follow congressional instructions when Congress chose to intervene. Corwin supported the Coudert resolution and said that Congress could not be reduced to a rubber stamp of presidential policies. It is a mark of the open-mindedness of Professors Commager and Schlesinger that, upon closer scrutiny of legal and constitutional materials in the context of the Vietnam War, they recanted their positions on presidential and congressional power and urged acknowledgment and enforcement of congressional control of the war power. ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_201","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_201","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41631","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41656","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_202","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_202","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41710","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41735","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_791","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_791","title":"The so-called “Great Debate” ended on an inconclusive note with a “sense-of-the-Senate” resolution in which the Senate approved Truman’s decision to deploy four additional divisions, but stated that no additional ground troops could be sent to Western Europe “without further congressional approval”—the last provision prevailing over administration opposition by a close vote of 49-43. Senator—and future president—Richard M. Nixon joined the group opposing the assertion of inherent presidential power and for the proposition that the deployment of troops is a congressional power. Senator Taft applauded the resolution as “a clear statement by the Senate that it has the right to pass on any question of sending troops to Europe to implement the Atlantic Pact, that it is unconstitutional for the President to send troops abroad to implement that pact without congressional approval, at least until war comes.” Secretary Acheson had dug in his heels on the issue of an expansive presidential power in foreign affairs and national security. He said the resolution was “without force of law” and that it “had in it a present for everybody.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Image_804","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_804","roles":{}},{"id":"Slide22414","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The \"Great Debate\"","thumbnail":"dr/Slide22414_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si81339","Image_145","btn_previous_201","si41631","si41656","btn_next_202","si41710","si41735","Text_Caption_791","Image_804"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide22414"}}},{"id":"si22586","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_105","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_105","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_98","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_98","title":"The Vietnam War ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_286","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_286","title":"Truman’s Precedent ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_287","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_287","title":"President Truman’s capacious view of the presidency inspired many who served in the Senate during the Korean War, including Senator Lyndon Johnson (D-TX). When Johnson inherited the presidency, he brought to the Oval Office a Trumanesque conception of the constitutional authority of the office: sweeping powers over foreign affairs and national security, executive prerogatives, and a broad understanding of the Commander in Chief Clause. The president, he was fond of saying, was the “Constitutional Leader.” “There are many, many who can recommend, advise, and sometimes a few of them consent,” he said in a speech in 1966. “But there is only one that has been chosen by the American people to decide.”  In the spring of 1965, President Johnson asserted his authority as Commander in Chief when he deployed  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_288","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_288","title":"22,000 American troops to the Dominican Republic. The ostensible purpose was the protection of American lives, but there was an underlying motive in the dispatch of an army many times larger than was necessary to serve the mission. Johnson’s real purpose, he disclosed was that “we don’t propose to sit here in our rocking chair with our hands folded and let the Communists set up any government in the Western Hemisphere.” Johnson never sought authorization from Congress; indeed, he contended that he did not need authority or support from Congress to promote the national security interests of the United States.  Johnson proceeded with the same level of confidence and conviction in deploying 500,000 troops to Vietnam, as he proclaimed the Indochina War to be “America’s War.” The war, of course, would last longer than any in American history and it would cause more American deaths in combat than any except the Civil War and the two world wars, and cost more money than any except the Second World War. His decision, a unilateral executive decision, was momentous. Where did he find precedent for his war? He found it in the assertions of unilateral presidential power advanced by the Truman Administration. 䬀䀲蘀㽬⨀䃧炀샭耀㸝Ȁ㼁䓀䃦풀서怀㷈䀀㹥ր䁱炀샭耀㸝嘀㼏䃛憠선 ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_203","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_203","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41789","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41814","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_204","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_204","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41868","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41893","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Slide22501","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The Vietnam War","thumbnail":"dr/Slide22501_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si22586","Text_Caption_286","Text_Caption_287","Text_Caption_288","btn_previous_203","si41789","si41814","btn_next_204","si41868","si41893"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide22501"}}},{"id":"si22671","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si22673","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_106","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_106","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_99","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_99","title":"The Gulf of Tonkin ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_289","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_289","title":"Retaliatory Actions ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_290","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_290","title":"The Gulf of Tonkin Resolution, rushed through Congress in 1964, was promoted in an atmosphere of misinformation and deceit. The resolution purported to authorize Johnson’s war in Southeast Asia, although Johnson claimed that he never needed congressional authorization for his military actions. In August 1964, after President Johnson reported two attacks on U.S. vessels in the Gulf of Tonkin, Congress quickly passed legislation to authorize the use of force. There were doubts at the time of the second attack, but Congress did not take the time to verify Johnson’s claims. In 2005, the National Security Agency publicly acknowledged that the alleged second attack never occurred.   On August 3, 1964, President Johnson announced that he had ordered retaliatory actions against the North Vietnamese for their operations in the Gulf of Tonkin. He spoke following an attack on the U.S. Destroyer Maddox, by Communist PT boats. On the following day he reported a second attack, this one against two American destroyers off the coast of North Vietnam. Johnson called in congressional leaders. Reminding himself, and members of Congress, of Senator Taft’s criticism of President Truman for not seeking congressional ratification of the Korean decision, Johnson asked their judgment about drafting something that “would give us the opinion of Congress.” The leaders liked the idea and the administration pulled out a draft resolution, prepared weeks before and awaiting passage. A special message to Congress on August 5 requested a joint resolution to affirm the United States’ determination to respond to North Vietnam’s attacks. He explained: “As I have repeatedly made clear, the United States intends no rashness and no wider war.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_205","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_205","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41947","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si41972","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_206","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_206","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si42026","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si42051","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Image_756","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_756","roles":{}},{"id":"Text_Caption_746","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_746","title":"USS Franklin D. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide22637","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The Gulf of Tonkin","thumbnail":"dr/Slide22637_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si22671","si22673","Text_Caption_289","Text_Caption_290","btn_previous_205","si41947","si41972","btn_next_206","si42026","si42051","Image_756","Text_Caption_746"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide22637"}}},{"id":"si22802","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si22804","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_108","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_108","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Image_146","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Image_146","roles":{}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_101","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_101","title":"Congressional Debates ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_293","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_293","title":"Little to None ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_294","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_294","title":"Congress spent little time debating the resolution. The House passed it unanimously. In the Senate, debate began on August 6 and concluded the next day, with only Senators Wayne Morse and Ernest Gruening voting no. Several senators raised penetrating questions. Senator Daniel Brewster said he would “look with dismay” at landing large armies in Southeast Asia and asked if anything in the measure would permit that. Senator Fulbright, the floor manager, agreed “that that is the last thing we would want to do,” but the language of the resolution did not prevent it.” Senator Morse predicted that “if we follow a course of action that bogs down thousands of American boys in Asia, the administration responsible for it will be rejected and repudiated by the American people. It should be.” Within a few years, the citizenry, sadly, would realize how prophetic Morse was. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide22717","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Congressional Debates","thumbnail":"dr/Slide22717_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si22802","si22804","Image_146","Text_Caption_293","Text_Caption_294","btn_previous_207","si42105","si42130","btn_next_208","si42184","si42209","Text_Caption_640","Image_606","Text_Caption_641","Image_607","Text_Caption_642","Image_608","Image_609","Image_610","Image_611","Button_498","si71055","si71081","Button_499","si71137","si71163","Button_500","si71219","si71245","Text_Caption_747"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide22717"}}},{"id":"si22960","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_109","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_109","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_102","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_102","title":"Constitutional Significance of the Gulf of Tonkin Resolution ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_298","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_298","title":"The constitutional significance of the resolution drafted by the administration left discerning thinkers in a quandary. Senator Gaylord Nelson objected: “there is no agreement in the Senate on what the joint resolution means.” Congress, the resolution provided, “approves and supports the determination of the President, as Commander in Chief, to take all necessary measures to repel any warmed attack against the forces of the United States and to prevent further aggression.” Did this mean efforts to prevent further aggression against U.S. troops, or further aggression anywhere? Was this a declaration of presidential power to use force without congressional authorization? Was Congress trying to delegate the war power to the president? The resolution declared that the “United States is, therefore, prepared, as the President determines to take all necessary steps, including the use of armed force, to assist any member of protocol state of the Southeast Asia Collective Defense Treaty (SEATO). The member and protocol states included Laos, Cambodia, ”the free territory of Vietnam,” Thailand, the Philippines,  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_299","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_299","title":"Australia, New Zealand, Great Britain, and France, among others. Thus, the president might deploy troops and engage in military force to support any of these nations who thought their defense was threatened.   Plainly, the Gulf of Tonkin Resolution represented an unconstitutional delegation of the war power. Congress, as we have seen, must decide on matters of war and peace. It is prohibited from transferring or delegating to the executive those constitutional powers— appropriations, law making and the war power—granted to it by the Constitution. Where the courts have held that Congress may delegate a “lesser power,” Congress is required to fix the standard and the policy to guide the exercise of the delegated power, but the resolution makes no attempt to supply a fixed standard to instruct or guide the president’s decision to use force, other than by his own will. As seen in our discussion of the War Powers Act of 1973, Congress is prohibited by the Constitution from transferring the war power to the president. Senator Nelson attempted to limit the authority granted by the resolution when he offered an amendment that President Johnson would seek “no extension of the present military conflict” and that “we should continue to attempt to avoid a direct military involvement in the southeast Asian conflict.” But Senator Fulbright refused to accept any amendments since, he believed, they would delay passage and would have to be settled in conference with the House. When Senator John Sherman Cooper asked whether, “if the President decided that it was necessary to use such force as he could lead into war, will we give that authority by this resolution?” Fulbright replied, “That is the way I would interpret it.” He added, with an air of hope, “I have no doubt that the President will consult with Congress in case a major change in present policy becomes necessary.” The vagueness of the language and the motives behind it spurred uncertainty and doubt about the vote to support the resolution. Many senators who voted for the resolution did not believe that they were authorizing an extended land war in Southeast Asia. And defenders of President Johnson did not suppose that Johnson, himself, was interested in a large, protracted war in 1964 when the resolution was passed.   In the end, LBJ’s request for the “opinion of Congress,” was of little importance, given his expansive view of presidential power to wage war. “We stated then,” he said in 1967 of his meeting with congressional leaders when he first raised the idea of a resolution, “and we repeat now, we did not think the resolution was necessary to what we did and what we’re doing.” The importance of the resolution to Johnson was purely political. “Part of being ready, to me,” he wrote in his memoirs, “was having the advance support of Congress for anything that might prove to be necessary. It was better to have a firm congressional resolution, and not need it, than some day to need it and not have it.” Johnson never believed that the resolution provided the legal authority for his war in Vietnam. ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_105","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_105","title":"The Commander in Chief Clause ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_307","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_307","title":"SEATO Treaty ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_308","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_308","title":"The Commander in Chief Clause has never been a source of unilateral executive war making. The Framers unanimously rejected the idea of vesting such authority in the president. The War Clause, moreover, was not qualified to permit the president to initiate war. The authority of the president to repel sudden attacks against the United States was limited to invasions of the United States; a presidential authority to initiate military strikes or war against a foreign nation which had suffered attacks, would vitiate the congressional power and vest it in the president. The SEATO Treaty, like the United Nations Treaty, and the North Atlantic Treaty Organization, specifically states that member nations are to answer requests for troops in accordance with their “constitutional processes.” In the United States, the “constitutional process” calls for Congress, alone, to make decisions on matters of war and peace. The president, then, may not unilaterally commit American forces to international organizations that seek troops. ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_310","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_310","title":"The Johnson Administration’s assertion of 125 precedents of executive war making, is the argument that presidential war making, if repeated often enough, acquires legal validity. History, it is said, has legitimated the practice of unilateral executive war making. The actual number of these episodes varies from list to list, as compilations have changed, but each of the incidents is said to constitute a legitimizing precedent for future presidential wars.   If practice establishes law, then the inescapable conclusion is that every commander of every military unit has the power to initiate war. What is perhaps most revealing about presidential understanding of the constitutional locus of the war power is that in the one or two dozen instances in which presidents have personally made the decision unconstitutionally to initiate acts of war, they have not purported to rely on their authority as Commander in Chief or Chief Executive. In all of these cases the presidents have made false claims of authorization, either by statute or by treaty or by international law. ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_311","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_311","title":"Powell v. McCormack ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_312","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_312","title":"It cannot be maintained that constitutional power, in this case the war power, can be acquired through practice. In Powell v. McCormack, in 1969, Chief Justice Earl Warren wrote, “that an unconstitutional practice has been taken before surely does not render that action any less unconstitutional at a later date.” In 1940, in Inland Waterways Corp. v. Young, Justice Felix Frankfurter, writing for a unanimous Court, echoed a centuries-old principle of Anglo-Saxon jurisprudence: “Illegality cannot attain legitimacy through practice.” These eminent jurists were probably familiar with the old English judge, who observed, wryly, that the practice of government is but feeble proof of its legality. In any case, the Supreme Court has repeatedly denied claims that the president can acquire power by a series of usurpations. If it were otherwise, the president might aggrandize all governmental power. Neither Congress nor the judiciary could lawfully restrain the exercise of the president’s accumulated constitutional powers. Clearly, this practice would scuttle our entire constitutional jurisprudence. Thus, the most recent act of usurpation stands no better than the first. Congress cannot divest itself of those powers conferred upon it by the Constitution, a necessary predicate of the separation of powers doctrine. Neither congressional abdication nor acquiescence can accomplish a transfer of power to the executive. As the Court held in United States v. Morton Salt, in 1950, harking back to an old axiom of English law, once powers are “granted, they are not lost by being allowed to lie dormant, any more than non-existent powers can be prescripted by an unchallenged exercise.” ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_221","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_221","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si43211","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si43236","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_646","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_646","title":"United States v. ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_317","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_317","title":"Does Money Equal Approval? ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_318","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_318","title":"Despite its assertion of an unqualified presidential power to wage war in Southeast Asia, the Johnson Administration argued that Congress had authorized the Vietnam War by funding it. Initially, federal judges accepted appropriations as sufficient authority and rejected claims to the contrary. A federal district judge, in Berk v. Laird (1970), observed: That some members of Congress talked like doves before voting with the hawks is an inadequate basis for a charge that the President was violating the Constitution in doing what Congress by its words had told him he might do.”  Experts advised the courts that appropriations bills do not encompass major declarations of policy. They explained that House and Senate rules are designed to prevent substantive legislation from being included in  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_319","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_319","title":"appropriations bills. Some courts continued to accept the theory that Congress may indirectly assent to war by funding it. When private parties argued that the repeal of the Tonkin Gulf Resolution on January 12, 1971, removed congressional authorization for the war in Vietnam, courts disagreed and pointed to the continued appropriation of funds.   Further study and understanding of the procedural distinction in Congress between the authorization and appropriation processes persuaded some judges that funding was not the legal equivalent of endorsing a policy. In Atlee v. Laird (1972), federal appellate judge, Arlan Adams, reasoned that it would be impossible to decide whether legislators, in voting on appropriations, actually intended to authorize military hostilities in Vietnam: “to explore these issues would require the interrogation of members of Congress regarding what they intended by their votes, and then synthesization of the various answers. To do otherwise would call for a gross speculation in a delicate matter pertaining to foreign relations.”  ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"btn_previous_223","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_previous_223","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si43369","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si43394","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"btn_next_224","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"btn_next_224","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si43448","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"si43473","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"}}},{"id":"Slide23523","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"Authorizing Hostilities through the Appropriation Power?","thumbnail":"dr/Slide23523_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si23608","Text_Caption_317","Text_Caption_318","Text_Caption_319","btn_previous_223","si43369","si43394","btn_next_224","si43448","si43473"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide23523"}}},{"id":"si23751","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"si23753","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_118","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_118","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_111","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_111","title":"The War Powers Act (1973) ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_322","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_322","title":"Clarifying the Difference Between Authorization and Appropriation ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_323","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_323","title":"The issue of whether Congress might, by an appropriation, authorize hostilities was further clarified by the War Powers Act of 1973. In Section 8(a), Congress precluded the inference. That section provides that authority to introduce U.S. troops into hostilities or imminent hostilities “shall not be inferred from any provision of law (whether or not in effect before the date of the enactment of this joint resolution), including any provision contained in an appropriation Act, unless such provision specifically authorizes the introduction of United States Armed Forces into hostilities or into such situations and states that it is intended to constitute specific statutory authorization within the meaning of this joint resolution…” This section should instruct presidents, courts, and the American citizenry on the difference between an authorization and an appropriation, when it comes to support for military hostilities. An explicit authorization of war is what the Framers had in mind when they placed the responsibility in Congress to engage in serious, solemn debate on the question of moving the nation from a state of peace to a state of war. Bush in the aftermath of the 9/11 outrage, represented a turning point in presidential aggrandizement of war and national security powers. There is little doubt that the terrorist attacks on September 11, 2001, exerted tremendous pressure on the American government to take quick, strong, and effective actions to protect the nation. Televised coverage of the hijacked planes flying into, and destroying, the north and south towers of the World Trade Center, with the loss of 3,000 lives, sent shock waves across the country. The additional news of another plane crashing into the Pentagon, and a fourth, destined for Washington, D.C., forced down by passengers in rural Pennsylvania, killing everyone on board, left officials and citizens alike fearing additional attacks. On September 12, President Bush told Attorney General John Ashcroft: “Don’t ever let this happen again.” The president pledged to win the war against terrorism—“whatever it takes,” he said, and without “lawyering this thing to death.” Administration officials understood that they would be blamed if another attack occurred on their watch; thus, the means by which the war on terrorism would be waged, would not be closely scrutinized, affirming the observation of Franklin Roosevelt’s Attorney General, Francis Biddle, that in times of war presidents aren’t terribly worried about the Constitution. ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_330","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_330","title":"National Security Strategy ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_331","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_331","title":"On September 17, 2002, the Bush Administration released a document called “National Security Strategy,” which articulated the doctrine of preemptive war and, in fact, the concept of preventive war. President Bush warned enemies seeking weapons of mass destruction, and vowed to “act against such emerging threats before they are fully formed.” History, he declared, “will judge harshly those who saw this coming danger but failed to act. In this new world we have entered, the only path to peace and security is the path of action.” Vice President Dick Cheney and other Bush officials amplified the determination to meet threats “before” they fully emerged, stating that the administration didn’t need to see the mushroom clouds before striking a potential foe.  There was, in National Security Strategy, the echo of the doctrine of preemptive war, a well-heeled doctrine at ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_332","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_332","title":"international law, firmly grounded in history, but alas, the Bush Administration’s position was much bolder. The concept of “preemptive” war was founded in the right of a nation to use military force in self-defense, that is, in anticipation of an immediate attack, such as the massing of troops on the border, an armada gathering at the coast, or clear, firm, and solid intelligence that an adversary is about to initiate hostilities with little time to deter the strike. Secretary of State Daniel Webster explained the doctrine under international law after the British, in 1837, sank an American ship, the Caroline, in United States waters because it was being used to supply insurrectionists against British rule in Canada. Webster acknowledged that a nation could use military force in the territory of another state, but only in those “cases in which the necessity of that self-defense is instant, overwhelming, and leaving no choice of means and no moment for deliberation.” Article 51 of the United Nations Charter permits the use of military force for self-defense, “if an armed attack occurs.” Presumably, the Charter embodies the right of a nation to strike preemptively; after all, why should a sovereign state be made to suffer military blows against its territorial integrity that it knows will be inflicted? Aside from the matter of constitutional standards, it is worthwhile to consider the implications of the influence of the Bush doctrine of preventive war for international peace and stability. The result would be chaos and, perhaps, nuclear war. President Dwight D. Eisenhower was once encouraged by an aide to embrace the concept of preventive war, but he immediately rejected the aide and his advice, warning that that doctrine was a recipe for disaster. ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Slide24275","class":"Normal Slide","instance":"The Supreme Court Rules on Executive Creation of Military Tribunals","thumbnail":"dr/Slide24275_thnail.jpg","children":["si6543","si24360","si24362","Image_148","Text_Caption_339","Text_Caption_340","btn_previous_239","si44633","si44658","btn_next_240","si44712","si44737","Image_402","Text_Caption_538","Image_403","Button_396","si60549","si60581","Text_Caption_793"],"roles":{"slide":{"durationInFrames":18000},"navigation":{"navid":"Slide24275"}}},{"id":"si24467","class":"TODO::Senthil","roles":{}},{"id":"Title_AutoShape_127","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Title_AutoShape_127","title":"Commander-in-Chief ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"SubTitle_AutoShape_120","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"SubTitle_AutoShape_120","title":"Guantanamo Bay ","roles":{"click":{"subtype":"button"},"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_341","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_341","title":"Beyond American Legal Restraints and Habeas Corpus ","roles":{"textData":{}}},{"id":"Text_Caption_342","class":"TODO::Senthil","instance":"Text_Caption_342","title":"When President Bush initiated the bombing strikes in Afghanistan in response to the 9/11 attacks, he notified Congress that he was acting on his own constitutional authority as Commander in Chief.
